For example, in the following code, <code>foo</code> is initialized during declaration, while <code>bar</code> is not.</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var foo = 1;\nvar bar;\n\nbar = 2;\n</code></pre>\n<p>This rule aims to bring consistency to variable initializations and declarations.</p>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/init-declarations
no-catch-shadow	A			Variables						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>In IE 8 and earlier, the catch clause parameter can overwrite the value of a variable in the outer scope, if that variable has the same name as the catch clause parameter.</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var err = &quot;x&quot;;\n\ntry {\n    throw &quot;problem&quot;;\n} catch (err) {\n\n}\n\nconsole.log(err)    // err is 'problem', not 'x'\n</code></pre>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule is aimed at preventing unexpected behavior in your program that may arise from a bug in IE 8 and earlier, in which the catch clause parameter can leak into outer scopes. This rule will warn whenever it encounters a catch clause parameter that has the same name as a variable in an outer scope.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-catch-shadow: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar err = &quot;x&quot;;\n\ntry {\n    throw &quot;problem&quot;;\n} catch (err) {\n\n}\n\nfunction err() {\n    // ...\n};\n\ntry {\n    throw &quot;problem&quot;;\n} catch (err) {\n\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-catch-shadow: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar err = &quot;x&quot;;\n\ntry {\n    throw &quot;problem&quot;;\n} catch (e) {\n\n}\n\nfunction err() {\n    // ...\n};\n\ntry {\n    throw &quot;problem&quot;;\n} catch (e) {\n\n}\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-catch-shadow
no-delete-var	A			Variables\nRecommended Rules						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>(recommended) The <code>&quot;extends&quot;: &quot;eslint:recommended&quot;</code> property in a configuration file enables this rule.</p>\n<p>The purpose of the <code>delete</code> operator is to remove a property from an object. Using the <code>delete</code> operator on a variable might lead to unexpected behavior.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule disallows the use of the <code>delete</code> operator on variables.</p>\n<p>If ESLint parses code in strict mode, the parser (instead of this rule) reports the error.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-delete-var: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar x;\ndelete x;\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-delete-var
no-label-var	A			Variables						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule aims to create clearer code by disallowing the bad practice of creating a label that shares a name with a variable that is in scope.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-label-var: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar x = foo;\nfunction bar() {\nx:\n  for (;;) {\n    break x;\n  }\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-label-var: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\n// The variable that has the same name as the label is not in scope.\n\nfunction foo() {\n  var q = t;\n}\n\nfunction bar() {\nq:\n  for(;;) {\n    break q;\n  }\n}\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-label-var
no-restricted-globals	A			Variables						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>Disallowing usage of specific global variables can be useful if you want to allow a set of global\nvariables by enabling an environment, but still want to disallow some of those.</p>\n<p>For instance, early Internet Explorer versions exposed the current DOM event as a global variable\n<code>event</code>, but using this variable has been considered as a bad practice for a long time. no-shadow	A			Variables						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>Shadowing is the process by which a local variable shares the same name as a variable in its containing scope. For example:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var a = 3;\nfunction b() {\n    var a = 10;\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>In this case, the variable <code>a</code> inside of <code>b()</code> is shadowing the variable <code>a</code> in the global scope. This can cause confusion while reading the code and it's impossible to access the global variable.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule aims to eliminate shadowed variable declarations.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-shadow: &quot;error&quot;*/\n/*eslint-env es6*/\n\nvar a = 3;\nfunction b() {\n    var a = 10;\n}\n\nvar b = function () {\n    var a = 10;\n}\n\nfunction b(a) {\n    a = 10;\n}\nb(a);\n\nif (true) {\n    let a = 5;\n}\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-shadow
no-shadow-restricted-names	A			Variables						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>ES5 15.1.1 Value Properties of the Global Object (<code>NaN</code>, <code>Infinity</code>, <code>undefined</code>) as well as strict mode restricted identifiers <code>eval</code> and <code>arguments</code> are considered to be restricted names in JavaScript. Defining them to mean something else can have unintended consequences and confuse others reading the code. For example, there's nothing prevent you from writing:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var undefined = &quot;foo&quot;;\n</code></pre>\n<p>Then any code used within the same scope would not get the global <code>undefined</code>, but rather the local version with a very different meaning.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-shadow-restricted-names: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nfunction NaN(){}\n\n!function(Infinity){};\n\nvar undefined;\n\ntry {} catch(eval){}\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-shadow-restricted-names: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar Object;\n\nfunction f(a, b){}\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-shadow-restricted-names
no-undef	A			Variables\nRecommended Rules						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>(recommended) The <code>&quot;extends&quot;: &quot;eslint:recommended&quot;</code> property in a configuration file enables this rule.</p>\n<p>This rule can help you locate potential ReferenceErrors resulting from misspellings of variable and parameter names, or accidental implicit globals (for example, from forgetting the <code>var</code> keyword in a <code>for</code> loop initializer).</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>Any reference to an undeclared variable causes a warning, unless the variable is explicitly mentioned in a <code>/*global ...*/</code> comment.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-undef: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar a = someFunction();\nb = 10;\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule with <code>global</code> declaration:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*global someFunction b:true*/\n/*eslint no-undef: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar a = someFunction();\nb = 10;\n</code></pre>\n<p>The <code>b:true</code> syntax in <code>/*global */</code> indicates that assignment to <code>b</code> is correct.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule with <code>global</code> declaration:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*global b*/\n/*eslint no-undef: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nb = 10;\n</code></pre>\n<p>By default, variables declared in <code>/*global */</code> are read-only, therefore assignment is incorrect.</p>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-undef
no-undef-init	A			Variables\nFixable Rules						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>(fixable) The <code>--fix</code> option on the <a href="../user-guide/command-line-interface#fix">command line</a> can automatically fix some of the problems reported by this rule.</p>\n<p>In JavaScript, a variable that is declared and not initialized to any value automatically gets the value of <code>undefined</code>. For example:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var foo;\n\nconsole.log(foo === undefined);     // true\n</code></pre>\n<p>It's therefore unnecessary to initialize a variable to <code>undefined</code>, such as:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var foo = undefined;\n</code></pre>\n<p>It's considered a best practice to avoid initializing variables to <code>undefined</code>.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule aims to eliminate variable declarations that initialize to <code>undefined</code>.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-undef-init: &quot;error&quot;*/\n/*eslint-env es6*/\n\nvar foo = undefined;\nlet bar = undefined;\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-undef-init: &quot;error&quot;*/\n/*eslint-env es6*/\n\nvar foo;\nlet bar;\nconst baz = undefined;\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-undef-init
no-undefined	A			Variables						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>The <code>undefined</code> variable is unique in JavaScript because it is actually a property of the global object. As such, in ECMAScript 3 it was possible to overwrite the value of <code>undefined</code>. While ECMAScript 5 disallows overwriting <code>undefined</code>, it's still possible to shadow <code>undefined</code>, such as:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">function doSomething(data) {\n    var undefined = &quot;hi&quot;;\n\n    // doesn't do what you think it does\n    if (data === undefined) {\n        // ...\n    }\n\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>This represents a problem for <code>undefined</code> that doesn't exist for <code>null</code>, which is a keyword and primitive value that can neither be overwritten nor shadowed.</p>\n<p>All uninitialized variables automatically get the value of <code>undefined</code>:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var foo;\n\nconsole.log(foo === undefined);     // true (assuming no shadowing)\n</code></pre>\n<p>For this reason, it's not necessary to explicitly initialize a variable to <code>undefined</code>.</p>\n<p>Taking all of this into account, some style guides forbid the use of <code>undefined</code>, recommending instead:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>Variables that should be <code>undefined</code> are simply left uninitialized.</li>\n<li>Checking if a value is <code>undefined</code> should be done with <code>typeof</code>.</li>\n<li>Using the <code>void</code> operator to generate the value of <code>undefined</code> if necessary.</li>\n</ul>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule aims to eliminate the use of <code>undefined</code>, and as such, generates a warning whenever it is used.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-undefined: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar foo = undefined;\n\nvar undefined = &quot;foo&quot;;\n\nif (foo === undefined) {\n    // ...\n}\n\nfunction foo(undefined) {\n    // ...\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-undefined: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar foo = void 0;\n\nvar Undefined = &quot;foo&quot;;\n\nif (typeof foo === &quot;undefined&quot;) {\n    // ...\n}\n\nglobal.undefined = &quot;foo&quot;;\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-undefined
no-unused-vars	A			Variables\nRecommended Rules						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>(recommended) The <code>&quot;extends&quot;: &quot;eslint:recommended&quot;</code> property in a configuration file enables this rule.</p>\n<p>Variables that are declared and not used anywhere in the code are most likely an error due to incomplete refactoring. Such variables take up space in the code and can lead to confusion by readers.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule is aimed at eliminating unused variables, functions, and parameters of functions.</p>\n<p>A variable is considered to be used if any of the following are true:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>It represents a function that is called (<code>doSomething()</code>)</li>\n<li>It is read (<code>var y = x</code>)</li>\n<li>It is passed into a function as an argument (<code>doSomething(x)</code>)</li>\n<li>It is read inside of a function that is passed to another function (<code>doSomething(function() { foo(); })</code>)</li>\n</ul>\n<p>A variable is <em>not</em> considered to be used if it is only ever assigned to (<code>var x = 5</code>) or declared.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-unused-vars: &quot;error&quot;*/\n/*global some_unused_var*/\n\n// It checks variables you have defined as global\nsome_unused_var = 42;\n\nvar x;\n\n// Write-only variables are not considered as used.\nvar y = 10;\ny = 5;\n\n// A read for a modification of itself is not considered as used.\nvar z = 0;\nz = z + 1;\n\n// By default, unused arguments cause warnings.\n(function(foo) {\n    return 5;\n})();\n\n// Unused recursive functions also cause warnings.\nfunction fact(n) {\n    if (n &lt; 2) return 1;\n    return n * fact(n - 1);\n}\n\n// When a function definition destructures an array, unused entries from the array also cause warnings.\nfunction getY([x, y]) {\n    return y;\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-unused-vars: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar x = 10;\nalert(x);\n\n// foo is considered used here\nmyFunc(function foo() {\n    // ...\n}.bind(this));\n\n(function(foo) {\n    return foo;\n})();\n\nvar myFunc;\nmyFunc = setTimeout(function() {\n    // myFunc is considered used\n    myFunc();\n}, 50);\n\n// Only the second argument from the descructured array is used.\nfunction getY([, y]) {\n    return y;\n}\n</code></pre>\n<h3>exported</h3>\n<p>In environments outside of CommonJS or ECMAScript modules, you may use <code>var</code> to create a global variable that may be used by other scripts. You can use the <code>/* exported variableName */</code> comment block to indicate that this variable is being exported and therefore should not be considered unused.</p>\n<p>Note that <code>/* exported */</code> has no effect for any of the following:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>when the environment is <code>node</code> or <code>commonjs</code></li>\n<li>when <code>parserOptions.sourceType</code> is <code>module</code></li>\n<li>when <code>ecmaFeatures.globalReturn</code> is <code>true</code></li>\n</ul>\n<p>The line comment <code>// exported variableName</code> will not work as <code>exported</code> is not line-specific.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for <code>/* exported variableName */</code> operation:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/* exported global_var */\n\nvar global_var = 42;\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-unused-vars
no-use-before-define	A			Variables						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>In JavaScript, prior to ES6, variable and function declarations are hoisted to the top of a scope, so it's possible to use identifiers before their formal declarations in code. This can be confusing and some believe it is best to always declare variables and functions before using them.</p>\n<p>In ES6, block-level bindings (<code>let</code> and <code>const</code>) introduce a &quot;temporal dead zone&quot; where a <code>ReferenceError</code> will be thrown with any attempt to access the variable before its declaration.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule will warn when it encounters a reference to an identifier that has not yet been declared.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-use-before-define: &quot;error&quot;*/\n/*eslint-env es6*/\n\nalert(a);\nvar a = 10;\n\nf();\nfunction f() {}\n\nfunction g() {\n    return b;\n}\nvar b = 1;\n\n// With blockBindings: true\n{\n    alert(c);\n    let c = 1;\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-use-before-define: &quot;error&quot;*/\n/*eslint-env es6*/\n\nvar a;\na = 10;\nalert(a);\n\nfunction f() {}\nf(1);\n\nvar b = 1;\nfunction g() {\n    return b;\n}\n\n// With blockBindings: true\n{\n    let C;\n    c++;\n}\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-use-before-define
callback-return	A			Node.js and CommonJS						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>The callback pattern is at the heart of most I/O and event-driven programming\nin JavaScript.</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">function doSomething(err, callback) {\n    if (err) {\n        return callback(err);\n    }\n    callback();\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>To prevent calling the callback multiple times it is important to <code>return</code> anytime the callback is triggered outside\nof the main function body. Neglecting this technique often leads to issues where you do something more than once.\nFor example, in the case of an HTTP request, you may try to send HTTP headers more than once leading Node.js to <code>throw</code>\na <code>Can't render headers after they are sent to the client.</code> error.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule is aimed at ensuring that callbacks used outside of the main function block are always part-of or immediately\npreceding a <code>return</code> statement. This rule decides what is a callback based on the name of the function being called.</p>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/callback-return
global-require	A			Node.js and CommonJS						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>In Node.js, module dependencies are included using the <code>require()</code> function, such as:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var fs = require(&quot;fs&quot;);\n</code></pre>\n<p>While <code>require()</code> may be called anywhere in code, some style guides prescribe that it should be called only in the top level of a module to make it easier to identify dependencies. For instance, it's arguably harder to identify dependencies when they are deeply nested inside of functions and other statements:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">function foo() {\n\n    if (condition) {\n        var fs = require(&quot;fs&quot;);\n    }\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>Since <code>require()</code> does a synchronous load, it can cause performance problems when used in other locations.</p>\n<p>Further, ES6 modules mandate that <code>import</code> and <code>export</code> statements can only occur in the top level of the module's body.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule requires all calls to <code>require()</code> to be at the top level of the module, similar to ES6 <code>import</code> and <code>export</code> statements, which also can occur only at the top level.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint global-require: &quot;error&quot;*/\n/*eslint-env es6*/\n\n// calling require() inside of a function is not allowed\nfunction readFile(filename, callback) {\n    var fs = require('fs');\n    fs.readFile(filename, callback)\n}\n\n// conditional requires like this are also not allowed\nif (DEBUG) { require('debug'); }\n\n// a require() in a switch statement is also flagged\nswitch(x) { case '1': require('1'); break; }\n\n// you may not require() inside an arrow function body\nvar getModule = (name) =&gt; require(name);\n\n// you may not require() inside of a function body as well\nfunction getModule(name) { return require(name); }\n\n// you may not require() inside of a try/catch block\ntry {\n    require(unsafeModule);\n} catch(e) {\n    console.log(e);\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint global-require: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\n// all these variations of require() are ok\nrequire('x');\nvar y = require('y');\nvar z;\nz = require('z').initialize();\n\n// requiring a module and using it in a function is ok\nvar fs = require('fs');\nfunction readFile(filename, callback) {\n    fs.readFile(filename, callback)\n}\n\n// you can use a ternary to determine which module to require\nvar logger = DEBUG ? require('dev-logger') : require('logger');\n\n// if you want you can require() at the end of your module\nfunction doSomethingA() {}\nfunction doSomethingB() {}\nvar x = require(&quot;x&quot;),\n    z = require(&quot;z&quot;);\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/global-require
handle-callback-err	A			Node.js and CommonJS						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>In Node.js, a common pattern for dealing with asynchronous behavior is called the callback pattern.\nThis pattern expects an <code>Error</code> object or <code>null</code> as the first argument of the callback.\nForgetting to handle these errors can lead to some really strange behavior in your application.</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">function loadData (err, data) {\n    doSomething(); // forgot to handle error\n}\n</code></pre>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule expects that when you're using the callback pattern in Node.js you'll handle the error.</p>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/handle-callback-err
no-mixed-requires	A			Node.js and CommonJS						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>In the Node.js community it is often customary to separate initializations with calls to <code>require</code> modules from other variable declarations, sometimes also grouping them by the type of module. This rule helps you enforce this convention.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>When this rule is enabled, each <code>var</code> statement must satisfy the following conditions:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>either none or all variable declarations must be require declarations (default)</li>\n<li>all require declarations must be of the same type (grouping)</li>\n</ul>\n<p>This rule distinguishes between six kinds of variable declaration types:</p>\n<ul>\n<li><code>core</code>: declaration of a required [core module][1]</li>\n<li><code>file</code>: declaration of a required [file module][2]</li>\n<li><code>module</code>: declaration of a required module from the [node_modules folder][3]</li>\n<li><code>computed</code>: declaration of a required module whose type could not be determined (either because it is computed or because require was called without an argument)</li>\n<li><code>uninitialized</code>: a declaration that is not initialized</li>\n<li><code>other</code>: any other kind of declaration</li>\n</ul>\n<p>In this document, the first four types are summed up under the term <em>require declaration</em>.</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var fs = require('fs'),        // &quot;core&quot;     \\n    async = require('async'),  // &quot;module&quot;   |- these are &quot;require declaration&quot;s\n    foo = require('./foo'),    // &quot;file&quot;     |\n    bar = require(getName()),  // &quot;computed&quot; /\n    baz = 42,                  // &quot;other&quot;\n    bam;                       // &quot;uninitialized&quot;\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-mixed-requires
no-new-require	A			Node.js and CommonJS						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>The <code>require</code> function is used to include modules that exist in separate files, such as:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var appHeader = require('app-header');\n</code></pre>\n<p>Some modules return a constructor which can potentially lead to code such as:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var appHeader = new require('app-header');\n</code></pre>\n<p>Unfortunately, this introduces a high potential for confusion since the code author likely meant to write:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var appHeader = new (require('app-header'));\n</code></pre>\n<p>For this reason, it is usually best to disallow this particular expression.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule aims to eliminate use of the <code>new require</code> expression.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-new-require: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar appHeader = new require('app-header');\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-new-require: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar AppHeader = require('app-header');\nvar appHeader = new AppHeader();\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-new-require
no-path-concat	A			Node.js and CommonJS						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>In Node.js, the <code>__dirname</code> and <code>__filename</code> global variables contain the directory path and the file path of the currently executing script file, respectively. Sometimes, developers try to use these variables to create paths to other files, such as:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var fullPath = __dirname + &quot;/foo.js&quot;;\n</code></pre>\n<p>However, there are a few problems with this. First, you can't be sure what type of system the script is running on. Node.js can be run on any computer, including Windows, which uses a different path separator. It's very easy, therefore, to create an invalid path using string concatenation and assuming Unix-style separators. There's also the possibility of having double separators, or otherwise ending up with an invalid path.</p>\n<p>In order to avoid any confusion as to how to create the correct path, Node.js provides the <code>path</code> module. This module uses system-specific information to always return the correct value. So you can rewrite the previous example as:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var fullPath = path.join(__dirname, &quot;foo.js&quot;);\n</code></pre>\n<p>This example doesn't need to include separators as <code>path.join()</code> will do it in the most appropriate manner. Alternately, you can use <code>path.resolve()</code> to retrieve the fully-qualified path:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var fullPath = path.resolve(__dirname, &quot;foo.js&quot;);\n</code></pre>\n<p>Both <code>path.join()</code> and <code>path.resolve()</code> are suitable replacements for string concatenation wherever file or directory paths are being created.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule aims to prevent string concatenation of directory paths in Node.js</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-path-concat: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar fullPath = __dirname + &quot;/foo.js&quot;;\n\nvar fullPath = __filename + &quot;/foo.js&quot;;\n\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-path-concat: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar fullPath = dirname + &quot;/foo.js&quot;;\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-path-concat
no-process-env	A			Node.js and CommonJS						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>The <code>process.env</code> object in Node.js is used to store deployment/configuration parameters. Littering it through out a project could lead to maintenance issues as it's another kind of global dependency. As such, it could lead to merge conflicts in a multi-user setup and deployment issues in a multi-server setup. Instead, one of the best practices is to define all those parameters in a single configuration/settings file which could be accessed throughout the project.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule is aimed at discouraging use of <code>process.env</code> to avoid global dependencies. As such, it will warn whenever <code>process.env</code> is used.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-process-env: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nif(process.env.NODE_ENV === &quot;development&quot;) {\n    //...\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-process-env: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar config = require(&quot;./config&quot;);\n\nif(config.env === &quot;development&quot;) {\n    //...\n}\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-process-env
no-process-exit	A			Node.js and CommonJS						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>The <code>process.exit()</code> method in Node.js is used to immediately stop the Node.js process and exit. This is a dangerous operation because it can occur in any method at any point in time, potentially stopping a Node.js application completely when an error occurs. For example:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">if (somethingBadHappened) {\n    console.error(&quot;Something bad happened!&quot;);\n    process.exit(1);\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>This code could appear in any module and will stop the entire application when <code>somethingBadHappened</code> is truthy. This doesn't give the application any chance to respond to the error. It's usually better to throw an error and allow the application to handle it appropriately:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">if (somethingBadHappened) {\n    throw new Error(&quot;Something bad happened!&quot;);\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>By throwing an error in this way, other parts of the application have an opportunity to handle the error rather than stopping the application altogether. If the error bubbles all the way up to the process without being handled, then the process will exit and a non-zero exit code will returned, so the end result is the same.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule aims to prevent the use of <code>process.exit()</code> in Node.js JavaScript. As such, it warns whenever <code>process.exit()</code> is found in code.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-process-exit: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nprocess.exit(1);\nprocess.exit(0);\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-process-exit: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nProcess.exit();\nvar exit = process.exit;\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-process-exit
no-restricted-modules	A			Node.js and CommonJS						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>Disallowing usage of specific Node.js modules can be useful if you want to control the available methods, a developer can\nuse, to implement a feature.</p>\n<p>This way you can block usage of the <code>fs</code> module if you want to disallow file system access.\nBlocking the <code>os</code> module can be useful if you don't want to allow any operating system specific code.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule allows you to specify modules that you don't want to use in your application.</p>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-restricted-modules
no-sync	A			Node.js and CommonJS						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>In Node.js, most I/O is done through asynchronous methods. However, there are often synchronous versions of the asynchronous methods. For example, <code>fs.exists()</code> and <code>fs.existsSync()</code>. In some contexts, using synchronous operations is okay (if, as with ESLint, you are writing a command line utility). However, in other contexts the use of synchronous operations is considered a bad practice that should be avoided. For example, if you are running a high-travel web server on Node.js, you should consider carefully if you want to allow any synchronous operations that could lock up the server.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule is aimed at preventing synchronous methods from being called in Node.js. It looks specifically for the method suffix &quot;<code>Sync</code>&quot; (as is the convention with Node.js operations).</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-sync: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nfs.existsSync(somePath);\n\nvar contents = fs.readFileSync(somePath).toString();\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-sync: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nobj.sync();\n\nasync(function() {\n    // ...\n});\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-sync

This rule\napplies to both array literals and destructuring assignments (ECMAScript 6).</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint-env es6*/\n\nvar arr = [ 'foo', 'bar' ];\nvar [ x, y ] = z;\n\nvar arr = ['foo', 'bar'];\nvar [x,y] = z;\n</code></pre>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule enforces consistent spacing inside array brackets.</p>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/array-bracket-spacing
block-spacing	A			Stylistic Issues\nFixable Rules						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>(fixable) The <code>--fix</code> option on the <a href="../user-guide/command-line-interface#fix">command line</a> can automatically fix some of the problems reported by this rule.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule enforces consistent spacing inside single-line blocks.</p>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/block-spacing
brace-style	A			Stylistic Issues\nFixable Rules						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>(fixable) The <code>--fix</code> option on the <a href="../user-guide/command-line-interface#fix">command line</a> can automatically fix some of the problems reported by this rule.</p>\n<p>Brace style is closely related to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indent_style">indent style</a> in programming and describes the placement of braces relative to their control statement and body. There are probably a dozen, if not more, brace styles in the world.</p>\n<p>The <em>one true brace style</em> is one of the most common brace styles in JavaScript, in which the opening brace of a block is placed on the same line as its corresponding statement or declaration. For example:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">if (foo) {\n  bar();\n} else {\n  baz();\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>One common variant of one true brace style is called Stroustrup, in which the <code>else</code> statements in an <code>if-else</code> construct, as well as <code>catch</code> and <code>finally</code>, must be on its own line after the preceding closing brace. For example:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">if (foo) {\n  bar();\n}\nelse {\n  baz();\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>Another style is called <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indent_style#Allman_style">Allman</a>, in which all the braces are expected to be on their own lines without any extra indentation. For example:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">if (foo)\n{\n  bar();\n}\nelse\n{\n  baz();\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>While no style is considered better than the other, most developers agree that having a consistent style throughout a project is important for its long-term maintainability.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule enforces consistent brace style for blocks.</p>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/brace-style
camelcase	A			Stylistic Issues						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>When it comes to naming variables, style guides generally fall into one of two camps: camelcase (<code>variableName</code>) and underscores (<code>variable_name</code>). This rule focuses on using the camelcase approach. If your style guide calls for camelcasing your variable names, then this rule is for you!</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule looks for any underscores (<code>_</code>) located within the source code. It ignores leading and trailing underscores and only checks those in the middle of a variable name. If ESLint decides that the variable is a constant (all uppercase), then no warning will be thrown. Otherwise, a warning will be thrown. This rule only flags definitions and assignments but not function calls. In case of ES6 <code>import</code> statements, this rule only targets the name of the variable that will be imported into the local module scope.</p>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/camelcase
capitalized-comments	A			Stylistic Issues\nFixable Rules						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>(fixable) The <code>--fix</code> option on the <a href="../user-guide/command-line-interface#fix">command line</a> can automatically fix some of the problems reported by this rule.</p>\n<p>Comments are useful for leaving information for future developers. In order for that information to be useful and not distracting, it is sometimes desirable for comments to follow a particular style. One element of comment formatting styles is whether the first word of a comment should be capitalized or lowercase.</p>\n<p>In general, no comment style is any more or less valid than any others, but many developers would agree that a consistent style can improve a project's maintainability.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule aims to enforce a consistent style of comments across your codebase, specifically by either requiring or disallowing a capitalized letter as the first word character in a comment. This rule will not issue warnings when non-cased letters are used.</p>\n<p>By default, this rule will require a non-lowercase letter at the beginning of comments.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/* eslint capitalized-comments: [&quot;error&quot;] */\n\n// lowercase comment\n\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">\n// Capitalized comment\n\n// 1. Non-letter at beginning of comment\n\n//  Non-Latin character at beginning of comment\n\n/* eslint semi:off */\n/* eslint-env node */\n/* eslint-disable */\n/* eslint-enable */\n/* istanbul ignore next */\n/* jscs:enable */\n/* jshint asi:true */\n/* global foo */\n/* globals foo */\n/* exported myVar */\n// eslint-disable-line\n// eslint-disable-next-line\n// https://github.com\n\n</code></pre>\n<h3>Options</h3>\n<p>This rule has two options: a string value <code>&quot;always&quot;</code> or <code>&quot;never&quot;</code> which determines whether capitalization of the first word of a comment should be required or forbidden, and optionally an object containing more configuration parameters for the rule.</p>\n<p>Here are the supported object options:</p>\n<ul>\n<li><code>ignorePattern</code>: A string representing a regular expression pattern of words that should be ignored by this rule. If the first word of a comment matches the pattern, this rule will not report that comment.\n<ul>\n<li>Note that the following words are always ignored by this rule: <code>[&quot;jscs&quot;, &quot;jshint&quot;, &quot;eslint&quot;, &quot;istanbul&quot;, &quot;global&quot;, &quot;globals&quot;, &quot;exported&quot;]</code>.</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li><code>ignoreInlineComments</code>: If this is <code>true</code>, the rule will not report on comments in the middle of code. By default, this is <code>false</code>.</li>\n<li><code>ignoreConsecutiveComments</code>: If this is <code>true</code>, the rule will not report on a comment which violates the rule, as long as the comment immediately follows another comment. By default, this is <code>false</code>.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Here is an example configuration:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-json">{\n    &quot;capitalized-comments&quot;: [\n        &quot;error&quot;,\n        &quot;always&quot;,\n        {\n            &quot;ignorePattern&quot;: &quot;pragma|ignored&quot;,\n            &quot;ignoreInlineComments&quot;: true\n        }\n    ]\n}\n</code></pre>\n<h4><code>&quot;always&quot;</code></h4>\n<p>Using the <code>&quot;always&quot;</code> option means that this rule will report any comments which start with a lowercase letter. This is the default configuration for this rule.</p>\n<p>Note that configuration comments and comments which start with URLs are never reported.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/* eslint capitalized-comments: [&quot;error&quot;, &quot;always&quot;] */\n\n// lowercase comment\n\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/* eslint capitalized-comments: [&quot;error&quot;, &quot;always&quot;] */\n\n// Capitalized comment\n\n// 1. Non-letter at beginning of comment\n\n//  Non-Latin character at beginning of comment\n\n/* eslint semi:off */\n/* eslint-env node */\n/* eslint-disable */\n/* eslint-enable */\n/* istanbul ignore next */\n/* jscs:enable */\n/* jshint asi:true */\n/* global foo */\n/* globals foo */\n/* exported myVar */\n// eslint-disable-line\n// eslint-disable-next-line\n// https://github.com\n\n</code></pre>\n<h4><code>&quot;never&quot;</code></h4>\n<p>Using the <code>&quot;never&quot;</code> option means that this rule will report any comments which start with an uppercase letter.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code with the <code>&quot;never&quot;</code> option:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/* eslint capitalized-comments: [&quot;error&quot;, &quot;never&quot;] */\n\n// Capitalized comment\n\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code with the <code>&quot;never&quot;</code> option:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/* eslint capitalized-comments: [&quot;error&quot;, &quot;never&quot;] */\n\n// lowercase comment\n\n// 1. Non-letter at beginning of comment\n\n//  Non-Latin character at beginning of comment\n\n</code></pre>\n<h4><code>ignorePattern</code></h4>\n<p>The <code>ignorePattern</code> object takes a string value, which is used as a regular expression applied to the first word of a comment.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code with the <code>&quot;ignorePattern&quot;</code> option set to <code>&quot;pragma&quot;</code>:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/* eslint capitalized-comments: [&quot;error&quot;, &quot;always&quot;, { &quot;ignorePattern&quot;: &quot;pragma&quot; }] */\n\nfunction foo() {\n    /* pragma wrap(true) */\n}\n\n</code></pre>\n<h4><code>ignoreInlineComments</code></h4>\n<p>Setting the <code>ignoreInlineComments</code> option to <code>true</code> means that comments in the middle of code (with a token on the same line as the beginning of the comment, and another token on the same line as the end of the comment) will not be reported by this rule.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code with the <code>&quot;ignoreInlineComments&quot;</code> option set to <code>true</code>:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/* eslint capitalized-comments: [&quot;error&quot;, &quot;always&quot;, { &quot;ignoreInlineComments&quot;: true }] */\n\nfunction foo(/* ignored */ a) {\n}\n\n</code></pre>\n<h4><code>ignoreConsecutiveComments</code></h4>\n<p>If the <code>ignoreConsecutiveComments</code> option is set to <code>true</code>, then comments which otherwise violate the rule will not be reported as long as they immediately follow another comment. This can be applied more than once.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code with <code>ignoreConsecutiveComments</code> set to <code>true</code>:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/* eslint capitalize-comments: [&quot;error&quot;, &quot;always&quot;, { &quot;ignoreConsecutiveComments&quot;: true }] */\n\n// This comment is valid since it has the correct capitalization.\n// this comment is ignored since it follows another comment,\n// and this one as well because it follows yet another comment.\n\n/* Here is a block comment which has the correct capitalization, */\n/* but this one is ignored due to being consecutive; */\n/*\n * in fact, even if any of these are multi-line, that is fine too.\n */\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code with <code>ignoreConsecutiveComments</code> set to <code>true</code>:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/* eslint capitalize-comments: [&quot;error&quot;, &quot;always&quot;, { &quot;ignoreConsecutiveComments&quot;: true }] */\n\n// this comment is invalid, but only on this line.\n// this comment does NOT get reported, since it is a consecutive comment.\n</code></pre>\n<h3>Using Different Options for Line and Block Comments</h3>\n<p>If you wish to have a different configuration for line comments and block comments, you can do so by using two different object configurations (note that the capitalization option will be enforced consistently for line and block comments):</p>\n<pre><code class="language-json">{\n    &quot;capitalized-comments&quot;: [\n        &quot;error&quot;,\n        &quot;always&quot;,\n        {\n            &quot;line&quot;: {\n                &quot;ignorePattern&quot;: &quot;pragma|ignored&quot;,\n            },\n            &quot;block&quot;: {\n                &quot;ignoreInlineComments&quot;: true,\n                &quot;ignorePattern&quot;: &quot;ignored&quot;\n            }\n        }\n    ]\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code with different line and block comment configuration:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/* eslint capitalized-comments: [&quot;error&quot;, &quot;always&quot;, { &quot;block&quot;: { &quot;ignorePattern&quot;: &quot;blockignore&quot; } }] */\n\n// capitalized line comment, this is incorrect, blockignore does not help here\n/* lowercased block comment, this is incorrect too */\n\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code with different line and block comment configuration:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/* eslint capitalized-comments: [&quot;error&quot;, &quot;always&quot;, { &quot;block&quot;: { &quot;ignorePattern&quot;: &quot;blockignore&quot; } }] */\n\n// Uppercase line comment, this is correct\n/* blockignore lowercase block comment, this is correct due to ignorePattern */\n\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/capitalized-comments
comma-dangle	A			Stylistic Issues\nFixable Rules						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>(fixable) The <code>--fix</code> option on the <a href="../user-guide/command-line-interface#fix">command line</a> can automatically fix some of the problems reported by this rule.</p>\n<p>Trailing commas in object literals are valid according to the ECMAScript 5 (and ECMAScript 3!) spec. However, IE8 (when not in IE8 document mode) and below will throw an error when it encounters trailing commas in JavaScript.</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var foo = {\n    bar: &quot;baz&quot;,\n    qux: &quot;quux&quot;,\n};\n</code></pre>\n<p>Trailing commas simplify adding and removing items to objects and arrays, since only the lines you are modifying must be touched.\nAnother argument in favor of trailing commas is that it improves the clarity of diffs when an item is added or removed from an object or array:</p>\n<p>Less clear:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-diff"> var foo = {\n-    bar: &quot;baz&quot;,\n-    qux: &quot;quux&quot;\n+    bar: &quot;baz&quot;\n };\n</code></pre>\n<p>More clear:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-diff"> var foo = {\n     bar: &quot;baz&quot;,\n-    qux: &quot;quux&quot;,\n };\n</code></pre>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule enforces consistent use of trailing commas in object and array literals.</p>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/comma-dangle
comma-spacing	A			Stylistic Issues\nFixable Rules						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>(fixable) The <code>--fix</code> option on the <a href="../user-guide/command-line-interface#fix">command line</a> can automatically fix some of the problems reported by this rule.</p>\n<p>Spacing around commas improve readability of a list of items. comma-style	A			Stylistic Issues\nFixable Rules						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>(fixable) The <code>--fix</code> option on the <a href="../user-guide/command-line-interface#fix">command line</a> can automatically fix some of the problems reported by this rule.</p>\n<p>The Comma Style rule enforces styles for comma-separated lists. func-style	A			Stylistic Issues						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>There are two ways of defining functions in JavaScript: <code>function</code> declarations and <code>function</code> expressions. Declarations contain the <code>function</code> keyword first, followed by a name and then its arguments and the function body, for example:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">function doSomething() {\n    // ...\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>Equivalent function expressions begin with the <code>var</code> keyword, followed by a name and then the function itself, such as:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var doSomething = function() {\n    // ...\n};\n</code></pre>\n<p>The primary difference between <code>function</code> declarations and <code>function expressions</code> is that declarations are <em>hoisted</em> to the top of the scope in which they are defined, which allows you to write code that uses the function before its declaration. For example:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">doSomething();\n\nfunction doSomething() {\n    // ...\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>Although this code might seem like an error, it actually works fine because JavaScript engines hoist the <code>function</code> declarations to the top of the scope. That means this code is treated as if the declaration came before the invocation.</p>\n<p>For <code>function</code> expressions, you must define the function before it is used, otherwise it causes an error. Example:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">doSomething();  // error!\n\nvar doSomething = function() {\n    // ...\n};\n</code></pre>\n<p>In this case, <code>doSomething()</code> is undefined at the time of invocation and so causes a runtime error.</p>\n<p>Due to these different behaviors, it is common to have guidelines as to which style of function should be used. There is really no correct or incorrect choice here, it is just a preference.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule enforces a particular type of <code>function</code> style throughout a JavaScript file, either declarations or expressions. You can specify which you prefer in the configuration.</p>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/func-style

keyword-spacing	A			Stylistic Issues\nFixable Rules						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>(fixable) The <code>--fix</code> option on the <a Block comments are not affected by this rule. By default, this rule ignores comments starting with the following words: <code>eslint</code>, <code>jshint</code>, <code>jslint</code>, <code>istanbul</code>, <code>global</code>, <code>exported</code>, <code>jscs</code>, <code>falls through</code>.</p>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/line-comment-position
linebreak-style	A			Stylistic Issues\nFixable Rules						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>(fixable) The <code>--fix</code> option on the <a href="../user-guide/command-line-interface#fix">command line</a> can automatically fix some of the problems reported by this rule.</p>\n<p>When developing with a lot of people all having different editors, VCS applications and operating systems it may occur that\ndifferent line endings are written by either of the mentioned (might especially happen when using the windows and mac versions of SourceTree together).</p>\n<p>The linebreaks (new lines) used in windows operating system are usually <em>carriage returns</em> (CR) followed by a <em>line feed</em> (LF) making it a <em>carriage return line feed</em> (CRLF)\nwhereas Linux and Unix use a simple <em>line feed</em> (LF). The corresponding <em>control sequences</em> are <code>&quot;\n&quot;</code> (for LF) and <code>&quot;\r\n&quot;</code> for (CRLF).</p>\n<p>Many versioning systems (like git and subversion) can automatically ensure the correct ending. However to cover all contingencies, you can activate this rule.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule enforces consistent line endings independent of operating system, VCS, or editor used across your codebase.</p>\n<h3>Options</h3>\n<p>This rule has a string option:</p>\n<ul>\n<li><code>&quot;unix&quot;</code> (default) enforces the usage of Unix line endings: <code>\n</code> for LF.</li>\n<li><code>&quot;windows&quot;</code> enforces the usage of Windows line endings: <code>\r\n</code> for CRLF.</li>\n</ul>\n<h3>unix</h3>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule with the default <code>&quot;unix&quot;</code> option:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint linebreak-style: [&quot;error&quot;, &quot;unix&quot;]*/\n\nvar a = 'a'; // \r\n\n\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule with the default <code>&quot;unix&quot;</code> option:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint linebreak-style: [&quot;error&quot;, &quot;unix&quot;]*/\n\nvar a = 'a', // \n\n    b = 'b'; // \n\n// \n\nfunction foo(params) { // \n\n    // do stuff \n\n}// \n\n</code></pre>\n<h3>windows</h3>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule with the <code>&quot;windows&quot;</code> option:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint linebreak-style: [&quot;error&quot;, &quot;windows&quot;]*/\n\nvar a = 'a'; // \n\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule with the <code>&quot;windows&quot;</code> option:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint linebreak-style: [&quot;error&quot;, &quot;windows&quot;]*/\n\nvar a = 'a', // \r\n\n    b = 'b'; // \r\n\n// \r\n\nfunction foo(params) { // \r\n\n    // do stuff \r\n\n} // \r\n\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/linebreak-style
lines-around-comment	A			Stylistic Issues\nFixable Rules						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>(fixable) The <code>--fix</code> option on the <a href="../user-guide/command-line-interface#fix">command line</a> can automatically fix some of the problems reported by this rule.</p>\n<p>Many style guides require empty lines before or after comments. The primary goal\nof these rules is to make the comments easier to read and improve readability of the code.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule requires empty lines before and/or after comments. It can be enabled separately for both block (<code>/*</code>) and line (<code>//</code>) comments. This rule does not apply to comments that appear on the same line as code and does not require empty lines at the beginning or end of a file.</p>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/lines-around-comment
lines-around-directive	A			Stylistic Issues\nFixable Rules						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>(fixable) The <code>--fix</code> option on the <a href="../user-guide/command-line-interface#fix">command line</a> can automatically fix some of the problems reported by this rule.</p>\n<p>Directives are used in JavaScript to indicate to the execution environment that a script would like to opt into a feature such as <code>&quot;strict mode&quot;</code>. Directives are grouped together in a <a href="http://www.ecma-international.org/ecma-262/7.0/#directive-prologue">directive prologue</a> at the top of either a file or function block and are applied to the scope in which they occur.</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">// Strict mode is invoked for the entire script\n&quot;use strict&quot;;\n\nvar foo;\n\nfunction bar() {\n  var baz;\n}\n</code></pre>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var foo;\n\nfunction bar() {\n  // Strict mode is only invoked within this function\n  &quot;use strict&quot;;\n\n  var baz;\n}\n</code></pre>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule requires or disallows blank newlines around directive prologues. This rule does not enforce any conventions about blank newlines between the individual directives. In addition, it does not require blank newlines before directive prologues unless they are preceded by a comment. Please use the <a href="padded-blocks">padded-blocks</a> rule if this is a style you would like to enforce.</p>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/lines-around-directive
max-depth	A			Stylistic Issues						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>Many developers consider code difficult to read if blocks are nested beyond a certain depth.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule enforces a maximum depth that blocks can be nested to reduce code complexity.</p>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/max-depth
max-len	A			Stylistic Issues						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>Very long lines of code in any language can be difficult to read. max-lines	A			Stylistic Issues						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>Some people consider large files a code smell. max-params	A			Stylistic Issues						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>Functions that take numerous parameters can be difficult to read and write because it requires the memorization of what each parameter is, its type, and the order they should appear in. multiline-ternary	A			Stylistic Issues						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>JavaScript allows operands of ternary expressions to be separated by newlines, which can improve the readability of your program.</p>\n<p>For example:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var foo = bar &gt; baz ? value1 : value2;\n</code></pre>\n<p>The above can be rewritten as the following to improve readability and more clearly delineate the operands:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var foo = bar &gt; baz ?\n    value1 :\n    value2;\n</code></pre>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule enforces or disallows newlines between operands of a ternary expression.\nNote: The location of the operators is not enforced by this rule. Please see the <a href="operator-linebreak">operator-linebreak</a> rule if you are interested in enforcing the location of the operators themselves.</p>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/multiline-ternary
new-cap	A			Stylistic Issues						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>The <code>new</code> operator in JavaScript creates a new instance of a particular type of object. That type of object is represented by a constructor function. Since constructor functions are just regular functions, the only defining characteristic is that <code>new</code> is being used as part of the call. Native JavaScript functions begin with an uppercase letter to distinguish those functions that are to be used as constructors from functions that are not. Many style guides recommend following this pattern to more easily determine which functions are to be used as constructors.</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var friend = new Person();\n</code></pre>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule requires constructor names to begin with a capital letter. Certain built-in identifiers are exempt from this rule. These identifiers are:</p>\n<ul>\n<li><code>Array</code></li>\n<li><code>Boolean</code></li>\n<li><code>Date</code></li>\n<li><code>Error</code></li>\n<li><code>Function</code></li>\n<li><code>Number</code></li>\n<li><code>Object</code></li>\n<li><code>RegExp</code></li>\n<li><code>String</code></li>\n<li><code>Symbol</code></li>\n</ul>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint new-cap: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nfunction foo(arg) {\n    return Boolean(arg);\n}\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/new-cap
new-parens	A			Stylistic Issues\nFixable Rules						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>(fixable) The <code>--fix</code> option on the <a href="../user-guide/command-line-interface#fix">command line</a> can automatically fix some of the problems reported by this rule.</p>\n<p>JavaScript allows the omission of parentheses when invoking a function via the <code>new</code> keyword and the constructor has no arguments. However, some coders believe that omitting the parentheses is inconsistent with the rest of the language and thus makes code less clear.</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var person = new Person;\n</code></pre>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule requires parentheses when invoking a constructor with no arguments using the <code>new</code> keyword in order to increase code clarity.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint new-parens: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar person = new Person;\nvar person = new (Person);\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint new-parens: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar person = new Person();\nvar person = new (Person)();\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/new-parens
newline-after-var	A			Stylistic Issues\nFixable Rules						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>(fixable) The <code>--fix</code> option on the <a href="../user-guide/command-line-interface#fix">command line</a> can automatically fix some of the problems reported by this rule.</p>\n<p>As of today there is no consistency in separating variable declarations from the rest of the code. Some developers leave an empty line between var statements and the rest of the code like:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var foo;\n\n// do something with foo\n</code></pre>\n<p>Whereas others don't leave any empty newlines at all.</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var foo;\n// do something with foo\n</code></pre>\n<p>The problem is when these developers work together in a project. This rule enforces a coding style where empty newlines are allowed or disallowed after <code>var</code>, <code>let</code>, or <code>const</code> statements. It helps the code to look consistent across the entire project.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule enforces a coding style where empty lines are required or disallowed after <code>var</code>, <code>let</code>, or <code>const</code> statements to achieve a consistent coding style across the project.</p>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/newline-after-var
newline-before-return	A			Stylistic Issues\nFixable Rules						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>(fixable) The <code>--fix</code> option on the <a href="../user-guide/command-line-interface#fix">command line</a> can automatically fix some of the problems reported by this rule.</p>\n<p>There is no hard and fast rule about whether empty lines should precede <code>return</code> statements in JavaScript. However, clearly delineating where a function is returning can greatly increase the readability and clarity of the code. For example:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">function foo(bar) {\n  var baz = 'baz';\n  if (!bar) {\n    bar = baz;\n    return bar;\n  }\n  return bar;\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>Adding newlines visibly separates the return statements from the previous lines, making it clear where the function exits and what value it returns:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">function foo(bar) {\n  var baz = 'baz';\n\n  if (!bar) {\n    bar = baz;\n\n    return bar;\n  }\n\n  return bar;\n}\n</code></pre>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule requires an empty line before <code>return</code> statements to increase code clarity, except when the <code>return</code> is alone inside a statement group (such as an if statement). In the latter case, the <code>return</code> statement does not need to be delineated by virtue of it being alone. Comments are ignored and do not count as empty lines.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint newline-before-return: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nfunction foo(bar) {\n    if (!bar) {\n        return;\n    }\n    return bar;\n}\n\nfunction foo(bar) {\n    if (!bar) {\n        return;\n    }\n    /* multi-line\n    comment */\n    return bar;\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint newline-before-return: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nfunction foo() {\n    return;\n}\n\nfunction foo() {\n\n    return;\n}\n\nfunction foo(bar) {\n    if (!bar) return;\n}\n\nfunction foo(bar) {\n    if (!bar) { return };\n}\n\nfunction foo(bar) {\n    if (!bar) {\n        return;\n    }\n}\n\nfunction foo(bar) {\n    if (!bar) {\n        return;\n    }\n\n    return bar;\n}\n\nfunction foo(bar) {\n    if (!bar) {\n\n        return;\n    }\n}\n\nfunction foo() {\n\n    // comment\n    return;\n}\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/newline-before-return
newline-per-chained-call	A			Stylistic Issues						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>Chained method calls on a single line without line breaks are harder to read, so some developers place a newline character after each method call in the chain to make it more readable and easy to maintain.</p>\n<p>Let's look at the following perfectly valid (but single line) code.</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">d3.select(&quot;body&quot;).selectAll(&quot;p&quot;).data([4, 8, 15, 16, 23, 42 ]).enter().append(&quot;p&quot;).text(function(d) { return &quot;I'm number &quot; + d + &quot;!&quot;; });\n</code></pre>\n<p>However, with appropriate new lines, it becomes easy to read and understand. Look at the same code written below with line breaks after each call.</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">d3\n    .select(&quot;body&quot;)\n    .selectAll(&quot;p&quot;)\n    .data([\n        4,\n        8,\n        15,\n        16,\n        23,\n        42\n    ])\n    .enter()\n    .append(&quot;p&quot;)\n    .text(function (d) {\n        return &quot;I'm number &quot; + d + &quot;!&quot;;\n    });\n</code></pre>\n<p>Another argument in favor of this style is that it improves the clarity of diffs when something in the method chain is changed:</p>\n<p>Less clear:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-diff">-d3.select(&quot;body&quot;).selectAll(&quot;p&quot;).style(&quot;color&quot;, &quot;white&quot;);\n+d3.select(&quot;body&quot;).selectAll(&quot;p&quot;).style(&quot;color&quot;, &quot;blue&quot;);\n</code></pre>\n<p>More clear:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-diff">d3\n    .select(&quot;body&quot;)\n    .selectAll(&quot;p&quot;)\n-    .style(&quot;color&quot;, &quot;white&quot;);\n+    .style(&quot;color&quot;, &quot;blue&quot;);\n</code></pre>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule requires a newline after each call in a method chain or deep member access. Computed property accesses such as <code>instance[something]</code> are excluded.</p>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/newline-per-chained-call
no-array-constructor	A			Stylistic Issues						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>Use of the <code>Array</code> constructor to construct a new array is generally\ndiscouraged in favour of array literal notation because of the single-argument\npitfall and because the <code>Array</code> global may be redefined. no-bitwise	A			Stylistic Issues						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>The use of bitwise operators in JavaScript is very rare and often <code>&amp;</code> or <code>|</code> is simply a mistyped <code>&amp;&amp;</code> or <code>||</code>, which will lead to unexpected behavior.</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var x = y | z;\n</code></pre>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule disallows bitwise operators.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-bitwise: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar x = y | z;\n\nvar x = y &amp; z;\n\nvar x = y ^ z;\n\nvar x = ~ z;\n\nvar x = y &lt;&lt; z;\n\nvar x = y &gt;&gt; z;\n\nvar x = y &gt;&gt;&gt; z;\n\nx |= y;\n\nx &amp;= y;\n\nx ^= y;\n\nx &lt;&lt;= y;\n\nx &gt;&gt;= y;\n\nx &gt;&gt;&gt;= y;\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-bitwise: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar x = y || z;\n\nvar x = y &amp;&amp; z;\n\nvar x = y &gt; z;\n\nvar x = y &lt; z;\n\nx += y;\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-bitwise
no-continue	A			Stylistic Issues						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>The <code>continue</code> statement terminates execution of the statements in the current iteration of the current or labeled loop, and continues execution of the loop with the next iteration. When used incorrectly it makes code less testable, less readable and less maintainable. Structured control flow statements such as <code>if</code> should be used instead.</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var sum = 0,\n    i;\n\nfor(i = 0; i &lt; 10; i++) {\n    if(i &gt;= 5) {\n        continue;\n    }\n\n    a += i;\n}\n</code></pre>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule disallows <code>continue</code> statements.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-continue: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar sum = 0,\n    i;\n\nfor(i = 0; i &lt; 10; i++) {\n    if(i &gt;= 5) {\n        continue;\n    }\n\n    a += i;\n}\n</code></pre>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-continue: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar sum = 0,\n    i;\n\nlabeledLoop: for(i = 0; i &lt; 10; i++) {\n    if(i &gt;= 5) {\n        continue labeledLoop;\n    }\n\n    a += i;\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-continue: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar sum = 0,\n    i;\n\nfor(i = 0; i &lt; 10; i++) {\n    if(i &lt; 5) {\n       a += i;\n    }\n}\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-continue
no-inline-comments	A			Stylistic Issues						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>Some style guides disallow comments on the same line as code. Code can become difficult to read if comments immediately follow the code on the same line.\nOn the other hand, it is sometimes faster and more obvious to put comments immediately following code.</p>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule disallows comments on the same line as code.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-inline-comments: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar a = 1; // declaring a to 1\n\nfunction getRandomNumber(){\n    return 4; // chosen by fair dice roll.\n              // guaranteed to be random.\n}\n\n/* A block comment before code */ var b = 2;\n\nvar c = 3; /* A block comment after code */\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-inline-comments: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\n// This is a comment above a line of code\nvar foo = 5;\n\nvar bar = 5;\n//This is a comment below a line of code\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-inline-comments
no-lonely-if	A			Stylistic Issues\nFixable Rules						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>(fixable) The <code>--fix</code> option on the <a href="../user-guide/command-line-interface#fix">command line</a> can automatically fix some of the problems reported by this rule.</p>\n<p>If an <code>if</code> statement is the only statement in the <code>else</code> block, it is often clearer to use an <code>else if</code> form.</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">if (foo) {\n    // ...\n} else {\n    if (bar) {\n        // ...\n    }\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>should be rewritten as</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">if (foo) {\n    // ...\n} else if (bar) {\n    // ...\n}\n</code></pre>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule disallows <code>if</code> statements as the only statement in <code>else</code> blocks.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-lonely-if: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nif (condition) {\n    // ...\n} else {\n    if (anotherCondition) {\n        // ...\n    }\n}\n\nif (condition) {\n    // ...\n} else {\n    if (anotherCondition) {\n        // ...\n    } else {\n        // ...\n    }\n}\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-lonely-if: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nif (condition) {\n    // ...\n} else if (anotherCondition) {\n    // ...\n}\n\nif (condition) {\n    // ...\n} else if (anotherCondition) {\n    // ...\n} else {\n    // ...\n}\n\nif (condition) {\n    // ...\n} else {\n    if (anotherCondition) {\n        // ...\n    }\n    doSomething();\n}\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-lonely-if
no-mixed-operators	A			Stylistic Issues						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>Enclosing complex expressions by parentheses clarifies the developer's intention, which makes the code more readable.\nThis rule warns when different operators are used consecutively without parentheses in an expression.</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">var foo = a &amp;&amp; b || c || d;    /*BAD: Unexpected mix of '&amp;&amp;' and '||'.*/\nvar foo = (a &amp;&amp; b) || c || d;  /*GOOD*/\nvar foo = a &amp;&amp; (b || c || d);  /*GOOD*/\n</code></pre>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule checks <code>BinaryExpression</code> and <code>LogicalExpression</code>.</p>\n<p>This rule may conflict with <a href="no-extra-parens">no-extra-parens</a> rule.\nIf you use both this and <a href="no-extra-parens">no-extra-parens</a> rule together, you need to use the <code>nestedBinaryExpressions</code> option of <a href="no-extra-parens">no-extra-parens</a> rule.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-mixed-operators: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar foo = a &amp;&amp; b &lt; 0 || c &gt; 0 || d + 1 === 0;\nvar foo = a + b * c;\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-mixed-operators: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar foo = a || b || c;\nvar foo = a &amp;&amp; b &amp;&amp; c;\nvar foo = (a &amp;&amp; b &lt; 0) || c &gt; 0 || d + 1 === 0;\nvar foo = a &amp;&amp; (b &lt; 0 || c &gt; 0 || d + 1 === 0);\nvar foo = a + (b * c);\nvar foo = (a + b) * c;\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-mixed-operators
no-mixed-spaces-and-tabs	A			Stylistic Issues\nRecommended Rules						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>(recommended) The <code>&quot;extends&quot;: &quot;eslint:recommended&quot;</code> property in a configuration file enables this rule.</p>\n<p>Most code conventions require either tabs or spaces be used for indentation. no-multi-assign	A			Stylistic Issues						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>Chaining the assignment of variables can lead to unexpected results and be difficult to read.</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">a = b = c = d;\n</code></pre>\n<h2>Rule Details</h2>\n<p>This rule disallows using multiple assignments within a single statement.</p>\n<p>Examples of <strong>incorrect</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-multi-assign: &quot;error&quot;*/\n\nvar a = b = c = 5;\n\nvar foo = bar = &quot;baz&quot;;\n\nvar a =\n    b =\n    c;\n</code></pre>\n<p>Examples of <strong>correct</strong> code for this rule:</p>\n<pre><code class="language-js">/*eslint no-multi-assign: &quot;error&quot;*/\nvar a = 5;\nvar b = 5;\nvar c = 5;\n\nvar foo = &quot;baz&quot;;\nvar bar = &quot;baz&quot;;\n\nvar a = c;\nvar b = c;\n</code></pre>\n</section>	http://eslint.org/docs/rules/no-multi-assign

no-negated-condition	A			Stylistic Issues						http://eslint.org/img/logo.svg	<section class="prog__container"><p>Negated conditions are more difficult to understand.
